    I'm glad to hear Wally Oppal is on the case. He is a good

    I'm glad to hear Wally Oppal is on the case. He is a good man -- smart, reasonable. Mediation and binding arbitration are two different things, GMP. I spoke out of turn when I said Carole Taylor is the only reasonable person there -- I forgot about Oppal.

    I don't belong to any political parties, never have -- check it out if you like. I am officially politically neutral. I have had some negative personal experiences with unions, which have shaped me and formed my personal opinions. Can we leave that aside for now?

    Oppal and Campbell are two different people. Forget Campbell for now. Forget Christy Clark. She played a huge part in souring relations. She's gone now. Bond is innocuous by comparison. Let's try to make some progress here.

    I have some experience as a mediator. A mediation is designed to isolate the issues, identify the facts that can be agreed upon, and bring the parties closer together, with the ultimate goal of reaching settlement. It is non-binding so that the parties can fully explore settlement possibilities (in arbitration the parties give evidence which is on the record).

    The first step is an agreed-upon mutual statement of facts. Facts are non-emotional, non-political, non-opinionated. I propose the following facts.

    1. The government legislated a 7% pay raise for teachers in 2002, but did not provide extra money to fund this raise. This necessitated school boards making cuts to fund the raise.

    2. Since 2002, there have been about 30 bargaining sessions between the parties. These all proved unsuccessful. No agreement has been reached.

    3. The government recently legislated a contract to which the teachers did not agree. The preamble to this legislation states that it is the product of collective bargaining. It is not.

    4. In response, teachers voted some 90% to take job action. Schools have been closed ever since.

    5. At issue are the cap on class sizes, funding for special needs students, and funding for ESL students. Teachers also seek a 15% pay raise.

    6. There is a great deal of acrimony between the parties. Relations have completely soured. Emotions are running very high.

    7. The Labour Board has declared that this an illegal job action, given that education is an "essential service" in B.C.

    8. The government has stated that it will not sit down with or speak to teachers, unless they go back to work first.

    9. The teachers have stated that they will not go back to work, until the government sits down with them to talk first.

    10. We have reached a complete stalemate.

    Can we agree on these facts? Does anybody have any other essential FACTS to add? If you agree, I could try and email Oppal with the list, and see if the government will agree that these are the facts.

    At least it is a first step.

    I do not believe the government will simply cave in, even in the face of a general strike, because to do so would set a precedent that would make their future governance impossible. "Escalation" is therefore not a realistic solution. It isn't possible to accurately predict the reaction of the public to an escalation, so given this uncertainty, perhaps a rational approach might be better.

    If anybody has any comments on this proposed agreed-upon statement of facts, please let me know. Your lawyers would charge you for this service -- I won't

    I've just read David Screck's blog. Usually, I think he's

    I've just read David Screck's blog. Usually, I think he's on the money but his take on the teacher's strike is increasingly bizarre. I wonder what he's up too?

    After suggesting, essentially, that Jim Sinclair mediate along the lines suggested here by Chella, he concludes his commentary with an observation which suggests that the Gov has no reason to mediate:

    "Ultimately the government will "win" and the teachers will be forced to return to work when the court bankrupts the union and, if necessary, imprisons its officers."

    This is utter nonsense, in my view. A whole lot of things that are very unlikely have to happen before the BCTF is bankrupted by court order, and an even more improbable sequence of events would have to occur before the officers of the BCTF could possibly be jailed for criminal contempt of court.

    But let's assume these things occured. 1. the BCTF, which is after all a federation of other associations- gets protection from its creditors. through banruptcy (unlikely) and 2. Jinny Sims and some other officers gets charged with something (unlikely), are denied bail pending that trial (unlikely in the extreme) and go to jail sometime in the future (very long odds). What possible impact would these possibilities have on the resolve of individuals teachers, and the associations that comprise the BCTF to carry on the fight? I can't see why it would lead to a collapse of resolve, to say the least.

    And yet, mostly liberals but now also Schreck keep asserting over and over again that this is all going to end with jailed officers of the BCTF (forgetting that they are entitled to trials, which take ages and rarely end with jail sentences for anything like this) and a bankrupted BCTF (forgetting that bankruptcy would benefit the BCTF by protecting it from its creditors). The Government does not want to prosecute the Jinny Sims trial, and they hardly want to arrange for the orderly payment of BCTF debts by applying to court to bankrupt it!

    David Schreck is being uncharacteristically superficial and might want to read up a bit before he advances the resistence is futile liberal spin.

    16-10-2005

    • Suggest as offensive

    Thank you for your response bloodnok. Learning is one

    Thank you for your response bloodnok.

    Learning is one thing, education is another.

    Learning is a lifelong process described by socrates as the gift of understanding.

    Education is the enforcement of bureaucratic and seemingly arcane rules of 'knowing'. Thus the enforced (albeit more loosely since the truancy laws are gone) schooling of youth in the prison-like atmosphere of modern industrial schools.

    Learning is natural, education is an industry.

    In response to the argument of private schools, they are only accessable to those whom can aford them in monetary terms, and homeschooling is only afforable to those with the time (as I know only too well with my own childrens' home schooling). Thus for the great mass these options are possibly out of reach.

    I personally would like to see a change in the methods and mindset that would allow homeschoolers to get their tax contribution back out of the system, I keep reading and hearing about numbers like $3000 to $5000 per year per student. Give me the money (via some sort of voucher system) and let me [the parent whom has the legal responsibility for the child in our system] make the decisions I am supposed to have the responsibility to make.

    Then allow the education industry to stand or fall on its own. If some teachers wish to organize and make a union or professional association then sell that to the voucher holders fine. If others want to stand apart and run smaller classes (10 students at $3000/year is a $30,000/year salary - plus deductions as a self-employed individual is a pretty great remuneration) and NOT be dictated to by a system or government or anyone else other than the parent/child clients that they choose to have the allow that method to stand along side the unionized one also.

    I agree that a learned society is one that will function well. I disagree that the public school system we have now serves us as the only way to achieve that learned society.

    I am most distressed that we seem incapable, as a society, to think differently. If we do the same things as in the past we shold not expect any different outcome than in the past.

    25 hours = 14 ideas for action; 1 concrete act. 161

    25 hours = 14 ideas for action; 1 concrete act.

    161 postings.

    Tomorrow I will be travelling to Victoria to participate in the demonstration. I will be leaving my local where I am acting as strike coordinator.

    I repeat my premise.

    Clarity of purpose is important; it's time for principled action.

    This blog can become a forum for taking political action, if you believe political action is important.

    Here's how.

    Post 1 example of a specific action you have taken or will take in the next 24 hours that you believe will have an effect on the parties involved and move negotiations forward.

    Remember, it's not about ideas for what others can do but ways forward you have taken.

    Simply to read of actions taken reafirms the power of collective action.

    Below is a list of actions suggested so far. You be the judge of their concreteness and effectiveness.

    1. Work collectively to approach financial institutions in the event of the denial of lines of credit or foreclosures.
    2. Collectively includes reaching out to non-union supporters to "chip in". To what or how this might be organized is still not clear but we're on our way.
    3. An appeal to local area residents and business owners to chip in. Good. An appeal by whom and how. Let's put some wheels on these.
    4. Great. Impromptu demonstations by students.
    5. Phone potential support on PAC (Parent Advisory Committee) and in the public. Give them at least one idea, such as writing letters/emailing/phoning to enourage a meeting. Or walking the line with us. Get THEIR phone trees started up.
    6.We can ask our school PACs to support and promote such efforts -- writing letters and organizing demonstrations of support on the picket lines (and I was not implying that all PACs were useless in my post above -- the key problem is BCCPAC's leadership or lack thereof --"SharingIsGood", your description fit to a T!).
    7.We can ask PACs and DPACs to pressure BCCPAC to take a stand as many of the school boards have done.
    8. We can ask the school boards that have not yet spoken up denouncing Bill 12 to do so.
    9.School trustees are facing re-election: we can ask sitting trustees and school board candidates to speak up re rescinding Bill 12 and urging government to meet with the teachers.
    10.Employers must be feeling the pinch of employee parents who are hamstrung by the strike, and the prospect of broader labour action -- encourage them to start putting pressure on the province.
    11. The Liberals will be polling quietly and monitoring the drift of letters published in local papers to gauge the public mood -- so keep sending letters to the papers.
    12. We should copy letters and e-mails to the Opposition Education critic (Jim Horgan?), as the Legislature will be sitting again next week.
    13.Parents can also network to support each other with child care, as the practical aspects are important to maintaining support if we're in for the long haul.
    14. Tie a green ribbon to your car aerial.

    Clarity of purpose is important; it's time for principled action.

  • bloodnok

    16-10-2005

    • Suggest as offensive
    • Recommend as a best comment

    Murdock Thanks for the explanation. I see your point about

    Murdock
    Thanks for the explanation. I see your point about the difference between learning and education and I agree; kids who are not in school at this very moment are still learning, about life, video games, the mall or whatever. But Socrates' idea of learning as the gift of understanding indicates that there is a giver and a receiver of said gift. This means that learning, natural though it be, must be directed in order to extend understanding into new areas. That is what education is all about. As has been mentioned earlier in a quote from Paolo Friere, there is no politically neutral education. Choices are always made about what is taught and how.

    A voucher system as you describe it seems perfectly reasonable, although I'm not sure how you would keep a voucher system from being corrupted to serve the needs of the few. I think where ever such a system has been tried, huge inequities have arisen, favouring those with more resources and influence. Also the small, accessible private schools you mentioned would likely be crippled economically in the face of larger education corporations, assuming the education industry goes the way of every other industry lately.

    I'm not saying that our present system is the only one we should pursue, just that it seems to meet the needs of the many in a practical way. I'm sure there are other systems out there where the same needs are met in other ways. The Dutch system seems to be pretty interesting. As I understand it, the state pays for all education no matter what the religious or social aim of the school is. Parents really like it, but there are costs. Money, of course, and a tendency to streamline kids into trades and professions at a very early age. I don't pretend to know the details but perhaps you could look into it as an alternative to promote.

    The "steeltoe" unions can negotiate agreements without that

    The "steeltoe" unions can negotiate agreements without that many strikes, while the Proffesional Associations(doctors, lawyers and teachers) and public sector unions have their contracts imposed by legislation

    I find this comment very interesting. The reason that trades unions can negoiate successfully is the concept of competition, something socialists hate. Say you are a skilled concrete former; if your boss does not give you a decent shake of things, you can walk across the street to an employer who will. My own employees got very generous bonuses this year because I simply cannot afford to lose them. My people have rejected a union every time they came around (and I make no attempt to stop them, they are such clowns I love to listen the them.) The rhetoric spewed by the union people when they come to my job sites is simply hilarious, so much that last time my people simply laughed them off the place.

    Then we have teachers. Little competition exists, altough it is quietly growing. They fee that they can defy the law because they have a monoploy of the service they provide. I have all kinds of rules and laws I have to obey in my business,some of which are inane and silly, but obey I must because it is the law.

    If I do not like that law, I can vote in a government I feel will change things more to my liking. If I knowingly break that law, I am open for punishment.

    Presently, I am doing a project for Coastal Health. These people are the epitome of regulations and rules. Can I pick and choose which ones I obey? Fat chance on that. If I want the job, I play the game. Why? Because if I want the work and to keep my people on that job, I have to follow the rules lest another company get the contract. That is competition.

    Probably the most comical part about working on a hospital project is lunch time. Yesterday was a very nice day (and because of that we were working overtime) and a few of my empoyees and I were sitting on the grass eating our sandwiches. A union rep came up and proceeded preach about class struggle and how their bosses were exploting the workers. One of my boys looked over to me and said "Gee, boss, I would love to be exploited by you!"

    With the resulting laughter, the union person ran away. I might add, I wear steel toes, work pants, a hard hat and Mac just like my workers do. The only difference is that I have the responsibility to make sure they continue to receive the high wages they do. A union won't do that for them.

    • Suggest as offensive

    There are some misconceptions being used in some of the

    There are some misconceptions being used in some of the arguments posted here.

    First, the BCTF does not have a monopoly on public education, the government does. The BCTF has a monopoly on representing teachers who work in the public school system. That is two very different things.

    Second, the College of Teachers has eight reps elected by teachers and eight reps appointed by government. The chair is also appointed by the government. This is hardly a BCTF controlled institution, they can't decertify teachers unless they violate the code of ethics. If you don't believe me, try BCCT.CA

    Third, teachers don't decide what to teach, that is a matter of government policy. As professionals, however, we do decide how to teach. After all, I don't pay an airline to fly me to Toronto and then proceed to tell the pilot how to fly the plane!

    some excellent comments today, esp from chella, who

    some excellent comments today, esp from chella, who actually is trying to separate fact from fiction (or, shall we say, overheated rhetoric). it's good to see oppal on the case - i DO believe the govt has to "stand down" on at least ONE thing, and perhaps this indicates some movement. we can only hope so.
    re: private schools - i was surprised upon moving to the Okanagan at just how many private schools there are up here - Waldorf, Montessori, the Xtian schools, etc. surprisingly, the one experiment in 'blue blood' style private schools - Central Okanagan Academy - went out of, uh, business last year just after constructing a brand new school. it now houses - public education irony, or what - a French school, for those from Quebec who live in the Okanagan or whose parents mother tongue is French.
    the faith-based schools - one just opened up, and its run by the Lutheran Church of Canada - hardly a bastion of hard-core evangelical thought - has an annual tuition per student of $1800. all the teachers are certified, and they follow the BC education curriculum. there is also, apparently, financial support available as well.
    however (this might shock some of you who think my previous postings have been right wing rants - alas, i've seen the excellent doc on the Winnipeg General Strike and dutifully learned about it in school) - the fact is we NEED public education to simply get kids from mixed social, religious, ethnic, and whatever else (disabilities) together to understand the cultural mosaic of Canadian society. i'm sorry, when i hear the term 'home schooling' the phrase 'wingnut' comes up next to it.
    that said, posters on this list would do well to read margaret wente's column on boys in the education system (in yesterday's Globe & Mail) and today's Norman Spector column - most importantly, the quote by Jack Munro after the Kelowna Accord was reached. i know that Munro pissed the coyotes of the world off with his 'capitulation,' but BC then was a very different place. it's MUCH better now (though, i'll admit, it still is a province that has problems with distribution of wealth), and Munro recognized that the province was on the brink of social (not to mention economic) anarchy, and pulled back. i think he recognized what the role of unions in society really is.
    now, i know that Canwest takes a thorough beating on these boards, but has anyone gone to the good unions at those newspapers and asked for THEIR support - virtually all of the Canwest journos i know likely support the teachers, and might show some solidarity. and again, what about teamsters, trades, and tech unions?

    In the decision quoted by Average Joe Madame Justice Brown

    In the decision quoted by Average Joe Madame Justice Brown said.

    Quote:
    [19] The impositions of sanctions pursuant to R. 56 is adjourned generally.

    If you are going to mess around with court orders make sure you understand them. She did not decide the punishment. Punishment was adjourned. Punishment is not "res judicata" and she is not "functus".

    In any event, the contempt is continuing. A new day is a new contempt and can give rise to new punishment.

    I am not trying to take sides in this issue. Negotiations have broken down. I expect that there is lots of blame on both sides. My experiences with teachers and with this government predisposes me to think that both are to blame.

    What I am seeing are posts about the law which are simply wrong. What I am trying to do is simply point out what the law is. If "Average Joe" or "John" have law degrees then you should be ashamed of the things you have posted about the law. If you do not have law degrees then you should stop playing lawyer. If you are parrotting things told to you by your lawyers, you need to get better lawyers.

So, there is NO POINT in telling teachers what to do in this forum.     "because I simply cannot afford to lose them."
    What would your workers be paid if there were no unions? Why don't you encourage them to join a union, so that you could pay them less?
    ... working conditions? ... safety?

    Homeschooling: Fine, if your child won't work at school (behaviour problem, etc.) and you can control that behaviour in a one-on-one situation. But unless the parents have the time, energy, teaching expertise, and the specialized knowledge of the teams of teachers in school systems, it all falls apart at about grade 8 or 9. The students either re-enter an educational institution at that time (often deprived of some aspects of normal schooling both academic and social), or they quit, and close the door on their chances to be educated for the modern working world. It is not reasonable to expect 2 parents (often one, if someone needs to work) to replace the educational opportunities provided by a diverse 30 to 40 member staff of secondary teachers, concentrating on instruction and learning, and nothing else, all day.

    Voucher system: An old, old, idea which has been tried repeatedly and has not worked.

    Chella: You've been duped. If "being in school" were the criterion for bargaining, the government has had years to bargain. They CAUSED this job action and now they want to be seen as stopping it. It's a PR crock.

    If teachers were to go back before written agreements are made, they lose, automatically. No raise, no change in conditions, and no bargaining rights. It is my opinion (and I don't suppose to tell anyone what to do) that if teachers were to go back before bargaining occurs, then all they have given up (I make it roughly $55,000,000 in personal pay alone) would have been for NOTHING.

    And how many registered apprentices are you carrying full

    In the great chasm that was the 1990's economically, we resorted to work sharing programmmes with our people to keep them employed and with us. Those were hard years but we learned how to be competitive. That really paid off when the economy picked up after 2001. If you are looking for shiny new trucks you will not see them with our company logo on them!

That's the secret of activism. Go to the individual businesses and ask them if they are even aware of what this Council is doing? Half the time, I wager they aren't or they haven't had time to check it out. Find out why they're members and handle that. Offer a better, more pragmatic alternative. That's key.
